{"parentUuid":"94bc492e-cc86-4d6f-8765-b52d2c3caad2","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"4cff8217-4055-4c23-8140-7e344f8eaa3c","agentId":"a492af28bc52d3613","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01KzjWxmZxzriyTbfVDKrBFu","type":"tool_result","content":"=== https://www.cognition.ai/blog/closing-the-agent-loop-devin-autofixes-review-comments ===\nCognition | Closing the Agent Loop: Devin Autofixes Review Comments devin Overview Enterprise Pricing Customers windsurf Overview Install Enterprise Pricing blog contact us government careers Try devin Menu Devin Overview Pricing Enterprise Customers Windsurf Overview Install Enterprise Pricing Blog Contact us Government Careers Try devin February 10, 2026 Closing the Agent Loop: Devin Autofixes Review Comments by The Cognition Team In this article: We built a feature that massively increased our internal token spend on Devin. But our PRs are now much more free of bugs and we can't go back. Two weeks ago, we built Devin Review , a new interface that helps you detect bugs and understand complex code in PRs. Why? Agents are generating code faster than teams can review them. The human bottleneck shifts from writing code to reviewing it. We found, however, that users would waste time copying & pasting between their coding agents and the review agent. Today, we're closing this loop. What we shipped Devin can now be configured to autofix incoming review comments from Devin Review and other review bots. Devin also continues to autofix lint and CI/CD issues. These move us a big step forward in closing the agent loop: writing and fixing its own code until its fully correct. When a GitHub bot comments on a PR - a linter flags an issue, CI catches a test failure, a security scanner surfaces a vulnerability - Devin can automatically pick it up and fix it. It works with any bot that comments on PRs. Linters, CI pipelines, security scanners, dependency managers - if it leaves a comment, Devin handles it. No human in the loop for mechanical fixes. Devin doesn't just flag problems, it resolves them. Then it feeds the fix back into the PR, creating a true feedback loop between the coding agent and the bug catcher. Why couldn't the code just be correct the first time? Even the best engineers might not catch everything on their first pass - you're focused on solving the problem, not stress-testing the solution. A review agent spends dedicated reasoning on the diff after it's written, and can go deep into specific issues not obvious just from the original plan. One agent writes, the other pressure-tests, and this continues in a loop. Write, catch, fix, merge The agent writes. The reviewer catches. Bot triggers fire. Fixes get applied automatically. CI runs clean. The PR is ready for human review. The human's job narrows to the decisions that require judgment: architecture, product direction, edge cases that need domain knowledge. Everything mechanical - the lint errors, the missed null checks, the off-by-one - gets caught and fixed before you even open the diff. A coding agent is a tool. A coding agent paired with a review agent that catches bugs, suggests fixes, and automatically resolves them through bot triggers - that's a system. Systems compound. Tools don't. There's still a gap: running the app, clicking through flows, writing unit tests. We're closing it. More soon. Getting started To enable bot triggers, go to Settings > Customization > Autofix settings and choose which bots Devin should respond to. To try Devin Review on any GitHub PR, replace github.com with devinreview.com in the URL. Both public and private PRs work without an account! Configure auto-review at app.devin.ai/settings/review and Devin starts reviewing every PR automatically - when they're opened, when commits are pushed, when reviewers are added. Or run it from your terminal: npx devin-review <https://github.com/owner/repo/pull/123> Try it on your next PR. This release is a significant step towards our vision for Devin to be the most autonomous agent: it's now able to test its work with computer use, self-verify, and auto-fix its code. Along with autonomy upgrades, we’ve been working on hundreds of improvements, big and small, to bring you a better experience. New users can get started for free with $10 in credits. End-to-end testing with computer use Devin has always been able to use a browser to record and test web apps. Now, with full access to its own Linux desktop, Devin can launch and test desktop applications too. After creating a PR, Devin will suggest testing it right on its desktop. Approve it, and Devin runs through your app and sends back screen recordings so you can review every detail of its work. Devin sessions for new users (as of Feb 24, 2026) will have Desktop support enabled by default. Existing users can turn on Desktop in Settings . Devin Review Autofix Devin doesn't just write code and hand it off. It plans, codes, reviews its own output, catches issues, and fixes them - all before you ever open the PR. The full loop, handled for you. Faster, Redesigned, and Built to Flow Devin now starts up 3x faster, so you move quicker from task to PR. And with a fully rebuilt interface that unifies every part of the development lifecycle — from planning to code review — it's easier than ever to understand and act on Devin's work. Get Started There's a lot more on the way. Try Devin today and see what's next. {"parentUuid":"d01d0d83-1e91-4ea3-9533-a52c1ab9d06b","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"e50e17d8-2302-40f1-8511-bee139f69f2b","agentId":"a492af28bc52d3613","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01TtL8WuDfZgL8UHJUXqDxPA","type":"tool_result","content":"=== https://githubnext.com/projects/copilot-workspace ===\nGitHub Next | Copilot Workspace Technical Preview Copilot Workspace An agentic dev environment, designed for everyday tasks. The Copilot Workspace technical preview is ending. The technical preview was sunset on May 30th, 2025. Learn more Getting started has never been simpler. Whether you’re addressing an Issue, iterating on a PR, or bootstrapping a project, Copilot Workspace helps you jumpstart every task by describing what you want in natural language. The plan agent captures your intent, proposes a plan of action, and uses that plan to implement the changes. Pivot at the speed of thought. Discuss ideas with Workspace’s brainstorm agent to understand the nuances of your solution, eliminate ambiguity, and consider alternatives. Everything in Workspace is designed to be edited, regenerated, or undone, so you can change your approach instantly, making it easy to iterate towards the exact solution you want. AI assistance that’s easy to verify. Validate your code changes using an integrated terminal and secure port forwarding. Test failure? Let Workspace’s repair agent fix it for you based on the error received. If you need the toolbox of a full IDE, you can open a Codespace and step through your code using the tools you already know. A workflow that’s collaborative by default. When you’re ready to get feedback, you can instantly share a workspace with your team, and they can try out their own iterations. Copilot Workspace automatically versions the context and history of your changes, and can create a PR in a single-click. Development you can do from anywhere. Using the GitHub mobile app, you can browse issues, repos, and PRs, and open them directly within Copilot Workspace. Explore ideas on the go, save your workspace to the dashboard, and pick it back up at your desk to add the finishing touches. Welcome to a new dev environment Frequently asked questions About “Copilot Workspace” or “Copilot Workspaces?” It’s Copilot Workspace — singular. That said, you should definitely have a few Copilot Workspace tabs open. For science. Who can access Copilot Workspace? Anyone with a paid Copilot Individual, Copilot Business, or Copilot Enterprise subscription can use Copilot Workspace. How do I get started? Check out our getting started guide ! Who appears as the author of a PR created with Copilot Workspace? You do! You're the pilot. You’re probably tired of hearing it, but it bears repeating: Copilot Workspace is a tool to help you write code, and you should always review and understand the code you’re proposing to others. Can others see the PRs I create with Copilot Workspace? Yes! PRs created with Copilot Workspace are normal PRs, and are accessible to anyone who would normally have access to PRs in the repository. Can I see Copilot Workspace in action before I get access? Every PR created with Copilot Workspace will include a comment which links to a read-only version of the Workspace that was used to create the PR. The read-only Workspace is accessible to collaborators with access to the repo, even if they have not been admitted to the technical preview. Can I share a Copilot Workspace session with others? Currently, you can share a snapshot of a Workspace with others. If they're admitted to the technical preview, they can fork your Workspace and iterate on it. If you make changes to a Workspace after sharing it, those changes will not be reflected in the shared version — you’ll have to share a new link with them. Some repositories don’t let me use Copilot Workspace. Why? Copilot Workspace uses OAuth for authentication. Some organizations can have policies which restrict OAuth applications from interacting with their repositories . You will not be able to use Copilot Workspace with those repositories unless the organization admin approves the Copilot Workspace OAuth application. 👋 Developer here! Can I start a Copilot Workspace session from an external tool? https://copilot-workspace.githubnext.com/ owner / repo ?task= description… P.S. we made a Raycast extension. How it works What models power Copilot Workspace? Right now, Copilot Workspace is powered by GPT-4o. We experimented with many different models from various providers, considering factors like power and latency, and determined GPT-4o is the best model for Copilot Workspace at this time. That said, we’ll continue to evaluate new models as they become available to ensure we’re always using the best model for the experience we want to deliver. What makes Copilot Workspace steerable? Copilot Workspace offers developers two important moments where they can steer the system via natural language: by altering the specification, and by altering the plan. When you prompt Copilot Workspace to make a change, it starts by reading your codebase and generating a specification. The spec is comprised of two bullet-point lists: one for the current state of the codebase, and one for the desired state. You can edit both lists, either to correct the system’s understanding of the current codebase, or to refine the requirements for the desired state. Once you’re happy with the spec, Copilot Workspace will generate a concrete plan for executing the changes. In this step, it will list out every file it intends to create, modify, or delete, as well as a bullet-point list of the actions to be taken in each file. Once again, you can edit any part of this plan — altering the specific instructions, or even changing the list of files that will be affected. Finally, the generated diff is itself directly editable, so you can tweak the code before creating a pull request or pushing the code to a branch. If you aren’t satisfied with the proposed changes, you don’t have to start over from scratch. Simply edit the spec or plan at any point, and Copilot Workspace will regenerate all the downstream steps with your new input. Why does steerability matter? Copilot’s inline suggestions are very easy to evaluate because they’re just a few lines long. When they aren’t correct, you can easily figure that out and ignore them. When you start to generate more code, that evaluation cost grows. You have to read more code, across more locations, and understand how it all fits together. If the suggestion isn’t quite right, it can feel frustrating — and the more code you try generate at once, the more likely it is that the suggestion will be incorrect in some way. Copilot Workspace is designed to break through that ceiling on suggestion size by mimicking how developers work through real problems. They start by articulating an understanding of how things work now, and how they would like them to work. Then, they identify where work needs to be done in order to reach the desired state. When you can steer the system at each of these steps, you are supplying crucial information that helps the model to generate code, and the resulting code is more likely to be correct. At the same time, you also make it easier for yourself to evaluate the correctness of the generated code, because you go into the review process with a clear expectation of which changes should happen where. How is Copilot Workspace different from Copilot and Copilot Chat? Copilot helps developers to write code by making suggestions as you type. Suggestions from Copilot are a few lines in length, which leads to a great experience for developers in the flow of writing code because those suggestions are easy to evaluate (and adjust where needed.) Copilot Chat is a great way to talk through potential changes, but it doesn't provide a rigorous, repeatable interaction pattern for applying that discussion to your codebase, especially when it would require changes to multiple files. Copilot Workspace is a task-oriented development environment. Instead of making suggestions as you type, Copilot Workspace helps you plan and author a coordinated set of changes — even changes that span multiple files in your repository, including changes that add or remove files. Jump to the section that matches your situation.\n\n  For an overview of model capabilities, see the  models overview . For details on what's new in Claude 4.6, see  What's new in Claude 4.6 . For migration guidance, see the  Migration guide .\n\n##         \n \n General principles\n\n###         \n \n Be clear and direct\n\n Claude responds well to clear, explicit instructions. Let me compile the full research report.\n\n---\n\n# Research Report: How Agent Skills Keep Agents On Track + User Engagement Patterns\n\n## PART 1: Deep Analysis of bladnman/ideation_team_skill\n\n**Repository:** https://github.com/bladnman/ideation_team_skill\n\n### 1.1 How It Structures Multi-Agent Dialogue\n\nThe skill orchestrates a team of 9 specialized agents through a structured creative process. The architecture is built on Claude Code's experimental **Agent Teams** feature (not subagents), which provides peer-to-peer messaging, shared task lists, and persistent sessions.\n\n**The structural hierarchy:**\n- **Arbiter (Team Lead)** -- coordinates, evaluates, signals convergence. Never generates ideas.\n- **Free Thinker** -- divergent generation, creative leaps, \"what if...\" proposals.\n- **Grounder** -- convergent editing, winnows ideas, keeps brainstorm on brief.\n- **Writer** -- synthesis and memory, observes dialogue, produces documents.\n- **Explorer** -- research agent, spawned on demand.\n- **Production agents** (Image, Presentation, Web Page, Archivist) -- conditional, only spawned based on user output selections.\n\n**The dialogue flow is enforced through communication topology:**\n- Free Thinker and Grounder communicate via `SendMessage` broadcasts (so the Writer can observe).\n- Idea reports go to the Arbiter via direct messages.\n- The Arbiter sends feedback (\"interesting\" / \"needs more conversation\" / \"not interesting\") back to the dialogue agents.\n- The Writer never participates in ideation -- only watches and synthesizes.\n\n**Critical design insight: cognitive separation.** The skill explicitly states that combining generation, evaluation, synthesis, and research in a single agent produces biased output. By separating these into distinct agents with strict role boundaries, each agent can operate without conflicts of interest.\n\n### 1.2 How It Prevents Skipping Steps\n\nThe skill uses a **layered enforcement strategy** -- a combination of hard structural gates, soft prompt discipline, and process-encoded checkpoints.\n\n**Hard Gates (structural enforcement):**\n\n1. **Mandatory Plan action before Ideate.** The SKILL.md states: \"Every new session starts with the Plan action. There is no way to skip the interview.\" The action detection logic parses the argument to route to Plan/Continue/PRD, and Plan always transitions into Ideate -- it cannot be bypassed.\n\n2. **AskUserQuestion gates at two points:**\n   - P4 (Interview): The Arbiter must present an `AskUserQuestion` to the user for depth level and output selection before proceeding.\n   - P6 (Confirm and Proceed): A second `AskUserQuestion` summarizes the configuration and requires explicit \"Start ideation\" confirmation.\n\n3. **Numbered step sequencing (P1-P6, I1-I4, C1-etc.):** Each action is decomposed into explicitly numbered phases with clear entry/exit conditions. The skill tells the agent exactly what to do at each step and when to transition to the next.\n\n4. **Task dependency blocking:** `TaskCreate` and `TaskUpdate` enforce sequencing -- e.g., \"First idea report\" is blocked by the initial exploration task. Production agents are blocked by upstream agents (Web Page blocked by Image Agent + Presentation Agent; Archivist blocked by Web Page).\n\n5. **Depth-aware convergence thresholds:** Hard numerical rules that vary by depth level. For \"Standard\" depth: minimum 3 idea reports before convergence check, forced convergence check at 6 reports, 2 of 4 criteria for \"interesting.\" These are stated as \"concrete behavioral rules, not advisory.\"\n\n6. **Delegate mode:** After setup, the Arbiter enters delegate mode (Shift+Tab), which physically restricts available tools to coordination-only (SendMessage, TaskCreate/Update/List, Read). This prevents the Arbiter from accidentally generating ideas or doing implementation work.\n\n**Soft Enforcement (prompt discipline):**\n\n1. **Explicit anti-patterns in each agent's prompt.** The Free Thinker is told: \"Do NOT self-censor. Do not pre-filter ideas for feasibility.\" The Grounder is told: \"You are NOT an analyst, a critic, or a technical reviewer.\" The Writer is told: \"You do NOT participate in ideation.\"\n\n2. **\"What good looks like\" and \"What to avoid\" sections** in every agent spawn prompt. These are concrete examples of desired and undesired behavior, not abstract principles.\n\n3. **The dialogue rhythm is prescribed:** \"After you propose or expand an idea, wait for the Grounder's response before continuing.\" This prevents one agent from dominating.\n\n4. **Depth directives are injected into each agent's prompt** with concrete behavioral rules calibrated to the session depth level. Not \"explore deeply\" but \"Spend 5-8 exchanges per promising direction.\"\n\n### 1.3 User Interaction via AskUserQuestion\n\nYes, the skill uses `AskUserQuestion` extensively and deliberately. There are precisely three user interaction points:\n\n1. **P4 Interview** -- 2-4 questions including depth level (Quick/Standard/Deep/Exhaustive), output selection (multiSelect), and conditional questions about domain clarification and research needs. If no concept was provided, an additional open-ended question asks for the concept.\n\n2. **P6 Confirmation** -- Summary of configuration with \"Start ideation\" / \"Adjust settings\" options. \"Adjust settings\" loops back to the relevant P4 question.\n\n3. **Continue action mini-interview** -- 2-3 questions about focus, depth, and optionally output selection for continuation sessions.\n\n**Key design principle:** The interview should feel like \"a quick pre-flight check, not an interrogation.\" The skill bundles questions into a single `AskUserQuestion` call rather than asking them serially. This minimizes user friction while maximizing information gathered.\n\n**Post-convergence user engagement:** The skill explicitly notes that after convergence, the user may want to push the system back into more work. Because Agent Teams teammates persist, the Arbiter can create new tasks and send new guidance. The team is NOT cleaned up until the user explicitly signals completion.\n\n### 1.4 Prompt Patterns That Keep Agents Following Structured Flow\n\nThe SKILL.md at 1,857 lines is itself the primary enforcement mechanism. Key patterns:\n\n**Pattern 1: Role-first identity anchoring.** Every agent prompt starts with \"You are the [Role] in multi-agent ideation\" followed by a bold one-line identity statement. This is the first thing the agent reads and it sets the frame for everything that follows.\n\n**Pattern 2: Numbered phase decomposition with clear transitions.** The entire skill is organized as Action 1 (Plan) -> Action 2 (Ideate) -> Action 3 (Continue) -> Action 4 (PRD), each with numbered sub-steps. The transitions are explicit: \"When the user confirms, transition directly into the Ideate action below.\"\n\n**Pattern 3: Teammate execution loop.** Every agent gets the identical 6-step loop: (1) Check TaskList, (2) Claim task, (3) Do work, (4) Mark complete, (5) Report via SendMessage, (6) Loop back. This creates a consistent behavioral backbone across all agents.\n\n**Pattern 4: Template-driven output.** Every output type has a template file the agent is told to read and follow: `idea-brief.md`, `idea-report.md`, `ideation-graph.md`, `vision-document.md`, `session-summary.md`, `session-config.yaml`, `lineage.md`, `prd.md`. This prevents agents from inventing their own output formats.\n\n**Pattern 5: Explicit \"Start by\" instructions.** Every spawn prompt ends with a concrete first action: \"Start by: Reading the concept seed, then broadcasting an opening message with your initial reactions.\" This eliminates the ambiguity of \"what do I do first?\"\n\n**Pattern 6: Anti-role contamination.** Each agent is told what it is NOT in addition to what it IS. The Grounder: \"You are NOT an analyst, a critic, or a technical reviewer.\" The Writer: \"You do NOT participate in ideation. You do not propose ideas, evaluate them, or steer the conversation.\"\n\n**Pattern 7: Convergence is emergent but bounded.** The skill says convergence is \"emergent, not declared\" but then immediately constrains it with numerical rules per depth level. This gives the feeling of natural flow while preventing runaway sessions.\n\n### 1.5 Hard Gates vs. Prompt Discipline\n\nThe skill uses **both**, but relies more heavily on prompt discipline than hard gates. The distribution is roughly:\n\n| Mechanism | Type | Count |\n|-----------|------|-------|\n| AskUserQuestion gates | Hard | 2-3 per session |\n| Task dependency blocking | Hard | 3-4 initial, dynamic thereafter |\n| Delegate mode tool restriction | Hard | 1 |\n| Depth threshold numbers | Semi-hard (prompt-enforced) | ~12 parameters per depth level |\n| Role identity anchoring | Soft | 9 agents |\n| Behavioral directives | Soft | Dozens per agent |\n| Template-driven output | Soft-to-semi-hard (file must be read) | 8 templates |\n| Execution loop pattern | Soft | Universal |\n\nThe skill achieves reliability through **density of instruction** rather than programmatic enforcement. The 1,857-line SKILL.md is so detailed and prescriptive that the agent has little room to deviate. When the prompt says \"do NOT create more than these initial tasks,\" that is prompt discipline, not a code constraint -- but it works because the instruction is unambiguous.\n\n### 1.6 What Wazir Can Learn From This Skill\n\n1. **The Plan action pattern is directly applicable to Wazir's workflow phases.** A mandatory pre-flight interview with `AskUserQuestion` that gathers scope, depth, and output expectations before any work begins. Wazir's clarifier already does some of this, but not with structured depth levels or output selection.\n\n2. **Depth-aware behavioral parameters.** Instead of a binary \"do it\" / \"do it thoroughly,\" the skill defines 4 depth levels with concrete numerical thresholds for every behavioral parameter. Wazir could adopt this for its review depth, test coverage expectations, and implementation thoroughness.\n\n3. **Cognitive role separation.** Wazir currently has roles but they are sequential (clarifier -> planner -> implementer -> reviewer). The ideation skill shows the value of running separate cognitive modes in parallel with explicit communication protocols between them.\n\n4. **Template-driven output contracts.** Every output type has a template that agents must read and follow. This is more reliable than describing the desired format in prose. Wazir's phase reports could benefit from template files.\n\n5. **The \"Start by\" pattern.** Every agent getting an explicit first action eliminates cold-start confusion. Wazir's role prompts could adopt this.\n\n6. **Post-completion persistence.** The team stays alive after convergence so the user can push back into more work. Wazir's workflows currently terminate at phase boundaries, losing context.\n\n---\n\n## PART 2: How Devin Shows Progress and Reasoning\n\n### 2.1 How Devin Shows What It's Doing and Why\n\nBased on the Cognition blog posts and product documentation:\n\n**Conversational interface as the primary UX.** Devin presents itself as a conversational teammate, not a tool. Users interact via Slack, Linear tickets, or the web app in the same way they would message a human colleague. The blog post \"How Cognition Uses Devin to Build Devin\" describes: \"we can chat with it the same way we'd message a teammate.\"\n\n**Real-time progress reporting.** From the original \"Introducing Devin\" announcement: \"Devin reports on its progress in real time, accepts feedback, and works together with you through design choices as needed.\" The key UX insight is that Devin does not go silent during long-running tasks -- it provides conversational updates.\n\n**Screen recordings for verification.** Devin 2.2 introduced full desktop access. After creating a PR, Devin suggests testing the app on its own Linux desktop, then sends back screen recordings so the user can visually verify the work. This turns \"trust me, it works\" into \"watch it work.\"\n\n**PR as the deliverable, not the conversation.** Devin's output is always a concrete artifact -- a PR, a report, a visualization. The conversation is the coordination layer; the PR is the proof of work. Every Devin PR in Slack includes a Devin Review link for organized diff viewing.\n\n### 2.2 Does It Explain Reasoning Before Acting?\n\n**Partially.** The Devin workflow for complex tasks involves:\n1. Analyzing the task (reading codebase, checking git history)\n2. Planning approach (automatically tailored prompts)\n3. Executing\n4. Self-reviewing (Devin Review autofix)\n5. Posting PR with explanation\n\nThe \"Ask Devin\" feature (codebase Q&A) is explicitly used for scoping work before starting sessions: \"use Ask Devin to explore the code and clarify a goal, then start a session directly from the search interface. Devin starts with clear context from our exploration.\" This suggests reasoning is shown during the planning phase but execution is more autonomous.\n\n### 2.3 How It Handles Long-Running Tasks\n\n**The \"agent loop\" pattern.** Devin 2.2 introduced a self-contained loop: write code -> review own output -> catch issues -> fix them -> all before the human opens the PR. This means the human's wait time produces a more polished result.\n\n**Asynchronous delegation model.** The Cognition team describes Devin as something you fire and forget: \"Tag @Devin with a request and we get a PR we can review and test out.\" The engagement model is not watching Devin work -- it is reviewing Devin's completed work.\n\n**Slack-native integration.** Because Devin lives in Slack, users get notifications when work is done, just like a teammate pinging them. This keeps users in their existing workflow rather than requiring them to monitor a separate dashboard.\n\n**Multi-Devin orchestration.** The March 2026 update added \"Devin can now Manage Devins\" -- breaking large tasks into subtasks delegated to parallel Devin instances. This addresses the \"feels too slow\" problem by parallelizing.\n\n### 2.4 Key UX Patterns from Devin\n\n1. **\"Teammate, not tool\" framing.** The user mental model is delegating to a colleague, not configuring a system.\n2. **PR-as-proof.** The deliverable is always a reviewable artifact.\n3. **Screen recordings for non-code verification.** Visual proof that the app works.\n4. **Autofix loop.** Write -> review -> fix -> repeat until CI passes. The human only sees the final result.\n5. **Low-friction entry.** Tag in Slack, mention in Linear, paste a URL. No special setup per task.\n6. **Non-engineers can contribute.** \"Anyone is able to contribute regardless of their technical expertise.\"\n\n---\n\n## PART 3: How GitHub Copilot Workspace Explains Steps\n\n### 3.1 How Copilot Workspace Shows Each Step\n\nCopilot Workspace uses a **spec-plan-implement pipeline** with full user visibility at each stage:\n\n1. **Specification.** The system reads the codebase and generates two bullet-point lists: (a) current state of the codebase, (b) desired state. Both lists are editable by the user.\n\n2. **Plan.** From the spec, it generates a concrete plan: every file it intends to create, modify, or delete, with bullet-point lists of actions per file. Fully editable.\n\n3. **Implementation.** Generates the actual diff/code changes. The diff is directly editable.\n\n4. **Validation.** Integrated terminal and secure port forwarding let the user run and test the changes.\n\n**The key insight from GitHubNext's documentation:** \"Everything in Workspace is designed to be edited, regenerated, or undone.\" This is not just transparency -- it is steerability at every stage.\n\n### 3.2 Does It Explain Reasoning?\n\nYes, through the specification stage. The spec is the system's reasoning made explicit: \"here is how I understand the current state, and here is what I think needs to change.\" By making this a separate, editable artifact, the user can correct misunderstandings before any code is written.\n\nThe GitHubNext FAQ explains why this matters: \"When you can steer the system at each of these steps, you are supplying crucial information that helps the model to generate code, and the resulting code is more likely to be correct. At the same time, you also make it easier for yourself to evaluate the correctness of the generated code, because you go into the review process with a clear expectation of which changes should happen where.\"\n\n### 3.3 How It Gets User Approval at Gates\n\nThere are **two explicit steer points** where the user can intervene:\n1. After the **specification** is generated (edit current/desired state).\n2. After the **plan** is generated (edit file list, per-file actions).\n\nAdditionally, the **brainstorm agent** can be invoked at any point to discuss ideas, eliminate ambiguity, and consider alternatives before proceeding.\n\nIf the user edits any upstream stage (spec or plan), all downstream stages are automatically regenerated. This creates a non-destructive feedback loop.\n\n### 3.4 Interactive or Autonomous?\n\n**Strongly interactive, with autonomous phases between gates.** The design philosophy is explicitly: \"Copilot Workspace is designed to break through that ceiling on suggestion size by mimicking how developers work through real problems.\"\n\nThe system is autonomous within each phase (generating a spec, generating a plan, generating code) but requires human review between phases. The human can also invoke a \"repair agent\" when tests fail, creating a semi-autonomous fix loop.\n\n**Copilot Workspace was sunset on May 30, 2025**, with its concepts folded into Copilot's core experience.\n\n---\n\n## PART 4: Agent Transparency Best Practices\n\n### 4.1 Anthropic's Recommendations\n\nFrom \"Building Effective Agents\" (Dec 2024), Anthropic articulates three core principles:\n\n> 1. Maintain **simplicity** in your agent's design.\n> 2. Prioritize **transparency** by explicitly showing the agent's planning steps.\n> 3. Carefully craft your **agent-computer interface** (ACI) through thorough tool documentation and testing.\n\n**The \"gate\" pattern.** In prompt chaining workflows, Anthropic recommends adding \"programmatic checks on any intermediate steps to ensure that the process is still on track.\" This is the hard gate concept.\n\n**Evaluator-optimizer loop.** One LLM generates, another evaluates and provides feedback in a loop. This maps directly to the ideation skill's Free Thinker / Grounder / Arbiter pattern.\n\n**Ground truth at every step.** For autonomous agents: \"it's crucial for the agents to gain 'ground truth' from the environment at each step (such as tool call results or code execution) to assess its progress.\"\n\n**Checkpoints for human feedback.** \"Agents can then pause for human feedback at checkpoints or when encountering blockers.\"\n\nFrom Anthropic's prompting best practices:\n- Claude 4.6 provides \"fact-based progress reports rather than self-celebratory updates\" -- the models are trained to be more grounded in their reporting.\n- If you want visibility into reasoning after tool use, you must explicitly request it: \"After completing a task that involves tool use, provide a quick summary of the work you've done.\"\n\n### 4.2 UX Patterns That Build Trust During Long-Running Tasks\n\nSynthesizing across all sources, the patterns that build trust are:\n\n**Pattern 1: Show the plan before executing it.**\n- Copilot Workspace: Spec -> Plan -> Code, each editable.\n- Ideation skill: Plan action interviews user, builds config, confirms before spawning team.\n- Devin: Ask Devin scoping before session start.\n\n**Pattern 2: Progress as narrative, not metrics.**\n- Devin reports in conversational Slack messages, not progress bars.\n- The ideation skill's Writer maintains a living ideation graph that shows how exploration is evolving.\n- Anthropic recommends \"fact-based progress reports\" -- what was done, what was found, what's next.\n\n**Pattern 3: Artifacts as proof of work.**\n- Devin produces PRs, screen recordings, review links.\n- Copilot Workspace shows editable diffs.\n- The ideation skill produces idea reports, snapshots, briefs, and a vision document.\n- Each artifact is reviewable independently of the process that created it.\n\n**Pattern 4: Steerability at defined moments.**\n- Users should be able to redirect at specific points without restarting.\n- Copilot Workspace: edit spec or plan, downstream regenerates.\n- Ideation skill: Arbiter sends \"needs more conversation\" to redirect exploration.\n- Devin: conversational back-and-forth during sessions.\n\n**Pattern 5: The deliverable should explain itself.**\n- The ideation skill's idea briefs contain lineage (how the idea evolved), the Free Thinker's vision, and the Grounder's take. The artifact carries its own reasoning.\n- Devin's PRs include Devin Review links for organized understanding.\n\n### 4.3 How to Make Users Feel Value Without Overwhelming Them\n\n**The \"newspaper front page\" principle.** Show the headline (what happened), have the article available (why and how), but don't force the user to read it. Concretely:\n\n1. **Top-level: one-sentence status.** \"Explored 4 directions, 2 marked interesting, entering convergence.\"\n2. **Mid-level: key decisions.** Available on request. The Arbiter's evaluations, the Grounder's take on each idea.\n3. **Deep level: full process record.** The ideation graph, snapshots, idea reports. Always available, never pushed.\n\n**The \"closed-loop notification\" pattern from Devin:**\n- Fire a task -> go do other work -> get notified when done -> review the artifact.\n- The user feels productive during the wait because they are not watching.\n- The notification arrives with the proof (PR link, review link), not just \"I'm done.\"\n\n**The \"versioned snapshots\" pattern from the ideation skill:**\n- The Writer produces snapshots at key moments (after first exchange, after first Arbiter evaluation, at major pivots, at convergence).\n- These are time-stamped checkpoints the user can review to understand how the session evolved.\n- Snapshot frequency scales with depth level (1-2 for Quick, 8+ for Exhaustive).\n\n---\n\n## PART 5: Concrete Patterns and Templates for Wazir\n\n### 5.1 Pattern: Pre-Flight Interview Gate\n\n**When to use:** Before any long-running workflow phase.\n\n**Template:**\n```\n## Pre-Flight Interview\n\nBefore beginning [phase name], gather the following via AskUserQuestion:\n\n1. **Scope/Depth**: [Options with time estimates and behavioral implications]\n2. **Expected Outputs**: [Predefined list + custom option]  \n3. **Conditional clarifications**: [Only if ambiguity detected in step analysis]\n4. **Confirmation**: [Summary of config + \"Start\" / \"Adjust\" options]\n\nRules:\n- Bundle all questions into a single AskUserQuestion call.\n- This interview CANNOT be skipped. It is the mechanism that prevents \n  launching into hours of work without user input on scope.\n- The interview should feel like a quick pre-flight check, not an interrogation.\n```\n\n### 5.2 Pattern: Depth-Aware Behavioral Parameters\n\n**When to use:** Any phase where thoroughness can vary.\n\n**Template:**\n```\n## Depth Levels\n\n| Parameter | Light | Standard | Thorough | Exhaustive |\n|-----------|-------|----------|----------|------------|\n| [Phase-specific metric 1] | [value] | [value] | [value] | [value] |\n| [Phase-specific metric 2] | [value] | [value] | [value] | [value] |\n| Review threshold | [value] | [value] | [value] | [value] |\n| Max iterations before forced checkpoint | [value] | [value] | [value] | [value] |\n\nThese are concrete behavioral rules, not advisory.\n```\n\n### 5.3 Pattern: Agent Role Prompt Structure\n\n**When to use:** Any role or agent spawn prompt.\n\n**Template:**\n```\nYou are the **[Role Name]** in [system context].\n\n**Your role is [one-sentence identity].** [Elaboration of what this means.]\n\n### How You Communicate\n[Exact communication channels and protocols]\n\n### How You Work\n[The execution loop -- numbered steps]\n\n### Your [Domain] Role\n[Detailed behavioral instructions]\n\n**What good looks like from you:**\n- [Concrete example 1]\n- [Concrete example 2]\n\n**What to avoid:**\n- [Anti-pattern 1]  \n- [Anti-pattern 2]\n\n**Start by:** [Explicit first action]\n\n{depth_directives}\n```\n\n### 5.4 Pattern: Progress Reporting Tiers\n\n**When to use:** Long-running tasks where the user needs to feel engaged.\n\n**Template:**\n```\n## Progress Reporting\n\n### Tier 1: Status Line (always shown)\nOne sentence: what phase we're in, what just completed, what's next.\nExample: \"Phase 2 of 4: Implemented 3 of 5 features. Starting auth module.\"\n\n### Tier 2: Key Decisions (shown at phase transitions)\nWhat choices were made and why. What was considered and rejected.\nExample: \"Chose SQLite over PostgreSQL because [reason]. Rejected in-memory \ncache because [reason].\"\n\n### Tier 3: Full Process Record (always written, never pushed)\nDetailed logs, diffs, test results. Available on demand.\nWritten to: {project-dir}/session/reports/\n\nRules:\n- After each tool use, provide a quick summary of what you did.\n- At phase transitions, summarize key decisions.\n- Never produce self-celebratory updates (\"Great news!\" / \"Excellent progress!\").\n- Use fact-based reporting: what was done, what was found, what's next.\n```\n\n### 5.5 Pattern: Artifact-as-Proof\n\n**When to use:** Any workflow that produces deliverables.\n\n**Template:**\n```\n## Deliverable Artifacts\n\nEvery phase produces artifacts that are reviewable independently of the \nprocess that created them.\n\n### Artifact Contract\nEach artifact must contain:\n1. What it is (self-describing header)\n2. How it was produced (brief lineage)\n3. What decisions shaped it (key reasoning)\n4. What's unresolved (open questions)\n\n### The Cardinal Rule: Err on Inclusion\nWhen in doubt, leave it in. It is better to include too much than to cut \nsomething that carried intent. The reviewer can trim; they cannot recover \nintent that was cut.\n```\n\n### 5.6 Pattern: Steerability Checkpoints\n\n**When to use:** Multi-step workflows where the user should be able to redirect.\n\n**Template (adapted from Copilot Workspace):**\n```\n## Steerability Design\n\n### Checkpoint 1: Specification\nSystem presents its understanding of current state and desired state.\nUser can edit both before proceeding.\n\n### Checkpoint 2: Plan  \nSystem presents concrete plan (files to change, actions per file).\nUser can edit any part. Downstream regenerates automatically.\n\n### Checkpoint 3: Review\nSystem presents completed work with artifacts.\nUser can accept, request changes, or redirect entirely.\n\n### Redirect Rule\nIf the user edits any upstream checkpoint, all downstream work is \nregenerated from that point. No manual cleanup required.\n```\n\n### 5.7 Pattern: Closed-Loop Async Delegation\n\n**When to use:** Tasks that take more than a few minutes.\n\n**Template (adapted from Devin):**\n```\n## Async Delegation Flow\n\n1. User provides task (natural language, in existing channel)\n2. Agent acknowledges with brief plan summary\n3. Agent executes autonomously\n4. Agent self-reviews and fixes issues\n5. Agent posts completed artifact (PR, report, etc.) with:\n   - One-sentence summary of what was done\n   - Link to detailed review\n   - Any questions requiring human judgment\n6. User reviews artifact at their convenience\n7. If changes needed, user responds in-thread (agent picks up and iterates)\n\nKey: The user should feel productive during the wait because \nthe notification arrives with proof, not just \"I'm done.\"\n```\n\n---\n\n## Summary of Key Findings\n\n| Source | Primary Enforcement | User Engagement Model | Key Innovation |\n|--------|--------------------|-----------------------|----------------|\n| **Ideation Skill** | Dense prompt discipline + few hard gates | Pre-flight interview + post-convergence persistence | Cognitive role separation across agents; depth-aware behavioral parameters |\n| **Devin** | Self-review loop (write/review/fix) | Async Slack delegation + PR-as-proof | \"Teammate not tool\" framing; screen recordings; non-engineers can contribute |\n| **Copilot Workspace** | Spec->Plan->Code pipeline with edit gates | Edit any stage, downstream regenerates | Steerability as trust mechanism; reasoning made into editable artifacts |\n| **Anthropic guidance** | Programmatic checks + tool documentation | Show planning steps explicitly | Simplicity first; ground truth at every step; ACI design |\n\n**The overarching principle across all sources:** Trust comes from making the agent's reasoning visible and editable, not from showing more output.
